  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8634f4a5-a5ac-461d-b666-05bd6537513c'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3855006852116895275">Considered by many to be one of Brecht's masterpieces, <i>Galileo</i> explores the question of a scientist's social and ethical responsibility, as the brilliant Galileo must choose between his life and his life's work when confronted with the demands of the Inquisition. Through the dramatic characterization of the famous physicist, Brecht examines the issues of scientific moralit</span>
  <span id="freeText3855006852116895275" style="display:none">Considered by many to be one of Brecht's masterpieces, <i>Galileo</i> explores the question of a scientist's social and ethical responsibility, as the brilliant Galileo must choose between his life and his life's work when confronted with the demands of the Inquisition. Through the dramatic characterization of the famous physicist, Brecht examines the issues of scientific morality and the difficult relationship between the intellectual and authority. This version of the play is the famous one that was brought to completion by Brecht himself, working with Charles Laughton, who played Galileo in the first two American productions (Hollywood and New York, 1947). Since then the play has become a classic in the world repertoire. "The play which most strongly stamped on my mind a sense of Brecht's great stature as an artist of the modern theatre was Galileo." - Harold Clurman; "Thoughtful and profoundly sensitive." - Newsweek.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3855006852116895275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1106698987" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1106698987" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1106698987?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/20128999-the-life-of-galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1106698987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16846270536411900142">"Unhappy the land where heroes are needed." <br/> <br/>History and literature studies have a natural connecting point in the human need for heroic action and (self-)sacrifice. Reading novelists, historians and poets who experienced the first and second world war first-hand, I slowly came to the conclusion that heroism itself is a flaw in human culture which rarely brings any benefits, but often creates suffering on a big scale, as heroes can be made instruments for dogma and set up as "martyrs" for a caus</span>
  <span id="freeText16846270536411900142" style="display:none">"Unhappy the land where heroes are needed." <br /> <br />History and literature studies have a natural connecting point in the human need for heroic action and (self-)sacrifice. Reading novelists, historians and poets who experienced the first and second world war first-hand, I slowly came to the conclusion that heroism itself is a flaw in human culture which rarely brings any benefits, but often creates suffering on a big scale, as heroes can be made instruments for dogma and set up as "martyrs" for a cause. And "causes", unfortunately, more often than not include violence and death. <br /> <br />How to live for freedom, truth and plurality of opinions if the majority of your community doesn't embrace your liberal curiosity, but believes in one single truth which needs to be protected at any cost?<br /><br />That is the question Galileo has to ask himself, and the question Brecht raised by choosing the topic during the darkest years of German oppression.<br /><br />Galileo is not a hero.<br /><br />He is not a religious martyr.<br /><br />He is not a perfect human being.<br /><br />He is independent, intelligent, inquisitive and inwardly free. He is interested in truth, but not in sacrifice to protect his truth from the institutionalised stupidity of his era, symbolised by the Inquisition and its pathetic struggle to keep the Bible an artificial authority in a world that increasingly builds on scientific facts and knowledge.<br /><br />Why does he recant? The question is not entirely answered, and there might not even be a straight forward answer to it. Brecht's Galileo claims that he is afraid of physical pain. And there is nothing shameful in that. Why suffer because of a doctrine which you know to be wrong? Whether Galileo is tortured or not, the world keeps moving. Eppure si muove, as he probably didn't really say.<br /><br />Being a scientist does not mean being a fanatic. You do not have to die to prove you are right. The experiments you conduct can be carried out again, with the same results - if your theory is correct. That is the main difference between ideology and science: the latter does not need heroes, because it can prove its point with reasonable, non-violent arguments and facts.<br /><br />Why is Galileo so unhappy then?<br /><br />Apart from being a scientist, he is also a human being living in an oppressive society, and with knowledge, you also acquire responsibility. As a teacher, you turn into a role model - not only for your subject, but for a recommendable behaviour within your community. And this is where he feels he has failed in the end. He should have made a point, stood up for his values and his discoveries, to set the stage for a future without oppressive ignorance in power. He didn't do that, and therefore he is unhappy.<br /><br />I still believe martyrdom is wrong. <br /><br />You won't win against dogma by using their means and their rhetoric, thus turning into the evil you want to fight. The only way to change the world is by educating the next generation NOT to believe in the ol' lie "dulce et decorum est pro patria mori", NOT to believe in having to "win" arguments against others, NOT to believe that there is only one solution to the great questions of humanity, NOT to believe that some people have the right to impose their worldview on others, NOT to believe that some people have more rights than others, NOT to believe that some people are entitled to privileges based on faith, looks, gender or other superficial distinctions. <br /><br />Education, in short. <br /><br />Galileo taught that torture is wrong by choosing not to suffer it. What he gained was more time to think and study and live. Inner emigration and civil disobedience are better means to fight oppression than violence, which always has a terror aspect.<br /><br />I could go on and on about this play, which left me shaken and confused all three times I read it (in high school, with my students, and on my own). Like Brecht's Galileo, I seem to change my mind on the "right" behaviour all the time, favouring different solutions depending on the changing political climate. <br /><br />Maybe we sometimes have to be heroes. But it should not be a career.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16846270536411900142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_242289281" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/242289281" />
    <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj"><img alt="Riku Sayuj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433433464p2/1651956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/242289281?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riku Sayuj" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj">Riku Sayuj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105381" href="/book/show/2525182.Life_of_Galileo">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Riku by: </span>
      Neerja Pandey
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=science-gen">science-gen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=r-r-rs">r-r-rs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer242289281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5149321163723364673">The play explores the pivotal moment in human history, at least in western history, when man confronts for the first time the proof that his conceptions of truth were entirely wrong.<br/><br/>Galileo comes alive as a larger than life genius from the pages, full of witticisms and blustering energy. Even his betrayal of his own science tends to be easily forgiven by the audience because he is such a genial revolutionary. <br/><br/>More than the drama of science standing up to the bully called religion, I liked the in</span>
  <span id="freeText5149321163723364673" style="display:none">The play explores the pivotal moment in human history, at least in western history, when man confronts for the first time the proof that his conceptions of truth were entirely wrong.<br /><br />Galileo comes alive as a larger than life genius from the pages, full of witticisms and blustering energy. Even his betrayal of his own science tends to be easily forgiven by the audience because he is such a genial revolutionary. <br /><br />More than the drama of science standing up to the bully called religion, I liked the instances of Marxism creeping into the play. In the discussions about Latin and how writing science in English will spell doom to the nobility, we get a sense that the real danger that Galileo represented was not just contradictory new knowledge but that the knowledge was suddenly out in the public realm. Galileo had to die because he was not just an academician, he was a new kind of preacher - a preacher of logic.<br /><br />These instances are woven into the grander drama with small scenes of Galileo ranting about professors having to teach all seven days and having not "time for research and about "knowledge as commodity", these are the scenes that to me made this a play of our times.<br /><br />The true gist of the play comes out in the penultimate scene. I would like to put some of it here so that even if someone does not have the patience to read the play, they can still get the spirit of its core argument. This occurs immediately after Andrei discovers that Galileo has been working on a scientific treatise even during his imprisonment:<br /><br /><blockquote>GALILEO: I had to do something with my time. <br />ANDREA: This will found a new science of physics. <br />GALILEO: Stuff it under your coat. <br />ANDREA: And we thought you had become a renegade! My voice was raised loudest against you! <br />GALILEO: And quite right, too. I taught you science and I denied the truth. <br />ANDREA: This changes everything, everything. <br />GALILEO: Yes? <br />ANDREA: You concealed the truth. From the enemy. Even in the field of ethics you were a thousand years ahead of us. <br />GALILEO: Explain that, Andrea. <br />ANDREA: In common with the man in the street, we said: he will die, but he will never recant. You came back: I have recanted, but I shall live. Your hands are tainted, we said. You say: better tainted than empty. <br />GALILEO: Better tainted than empty. Sounds realistic. Sounds like me. New science, new ethics. <br />ANDREA: I of all people ought to have known. I was eleven years old when you sold another man’s telescope to the Venetian Senate. And I saw you make immortal use of that instrument. Your friends shook their heads when you bowed before a child in Florence, but science caught the public fancy. You always laughed at our heroes. “People that suffer bore me,’ you said. ‘Misfortune comes from insufficient foresight.’ And: Taking obstacles into account, the shortest line between two points may be a crooked one.” <br />GALILEO: I recollect. <br />ANDREA: Then, in 1633, when it suited you to retract a popular point in your teachings, I should have known that you were only withdrawing from a hopeless political squabble in order to be able to carry on with your real business of science. <br />GALILEO: Which consists in ... <br />ANDREA: . . . The study of the properties of motion, mother of machines, which will make the earth so inhabitable that heaven can be demolished. <br />GALILEO : Aha. <br />ANDREA: You thereby gained the leisure to write a scientific work which only you could write. Had you ended in a halo of flames at the stake, the others would have been the victors. <br />GALILEO: They are the victors. And there is no scientific work which only one man can write. <br />ANDREA: Then why did you recant? <br />GALILEO: I recanted because I was afraid of physical pain. <br />ANDREA: No! <br />GALILEO: I was shown the instruments. <br />ANDREA: So there was no plan? <br />GALILEO: There was none.</blockquote><br /><br />Definitely a play worth reading, not for a scientific or historic perspective but for a picture of how reason and logic broke free from dogma and of how one man made the whole world tremble by unfolding a telescope!<br /><br />It is indeed a marvelous portrait of intellectual betrayal. The angry impotence of a man who realizes that he is ethically unequipped to deal with the consequences of his own genius.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5149321163723364673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1558937681" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1558937681" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1558937681?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783518100011" href="/book/show/241526.Leben_des_Galilei">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=cosmos">cosmos</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=renaissance">renaissance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=20-century">20-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1558937681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16407451517264507277"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459272245i/18590268._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Recently I attended a production of this play translated into Spanish and adapted in a striking way. The vague notions I have of Brecht’s idea of the theatre, the Epic theatre, did not seem to be staged in this performance. There was no <i>Verfremdung</i>. On the contrary, the theatre hall had been transformed and the stage was in the middle and had a circular platform that rotated, and we the audience were siting around it. Various lights and shadows and images were projected onto its floor-screen. A </span>
  <span id="freeText16407451517264507277" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459272245i/18590268._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Recently I attended a production of this play translated into Spanish and adapted in a striking way. The vague notions I have of Brecht’s idea of the theatre, the Epic theatre, did not seem to be staged in this performance. There was no <i>Verfremdung</i>. On the contrary, the theatre hall had been transformed and the stage was in the middle and had a circular platform that rotated, and we the audience were siting around it. Various lights and shadows and images were projected onto its floor-screen. A presentation entirely fitting to the cosmological concerns of the play, but which seemed to incorporate the audience by bringing it in, an effect reinforced by having the characters also walk outside the stage proper. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458668668i/18516835._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Another modification was that Bertolt Brecht himself appeared on the scene. I mean an actor playing Brecht, of course. He arrives at the beginning to supervise a Spanish troupe that is going to perform his play. The simultaneous dialogue in German and Spanish created a somewhat comical scene, which again had the opposite effect from <i>Verfremdung</i>. Or may be that is what Brecht wanted, since his objective was that the audience would never forget that they were assisting to a representation and not witnessing reality itself. <br><br>The character playing Brecht then decided to play Galileo himself. <br><br>The production maintained the musical ingredient, originally by <b>Hanns Eisler</b>, but I cannot vouch whether that was the version I heard. We had a singer with a wide voice range of voices, since he could move from a high baritone to a counter-tenor pitch. The incidental music was very effective and helped to set the different moods as the play unfolded.<br><br>As the stage, apart from the images screened on the floor was rather sparse, another remarkable invention to suggest an Italian setting was when various actors suddenly froze and posed as various well known figures around the circled stage. Most of them were by Michael Angelo, his <i>David</i>, <i>Moses</i>, <i>Pietà</i>, but there was also the <i>Roman Boy with the Thorn</i>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458668668i/18516836._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>After attending the performance, I read the play and it is then that I recognized more of the Epic theatre aims of Brecht. Instead of acts, he has fifteen frames and there are few stage indications. The play lets itself be comfortably read, like an epic.<br><br>And of course, one could make a parallel between the story of Galileo during the last years of his life, when he was confronted by the (Catholic) authorities, to the one that Brecht had to endure during his exiled years in the US, when he was associated with Communism. Brecht left the country; Galileo suffered house arrest.<br><br><br>******<br><br>Performance at the Valle-Inclán theatre, in Madrid.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="16407451517264507277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1042239055" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1042239055" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1042239055?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=history-and-biography">history-and-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science">science</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=linguistics-and-philosophy">linguistics-and-philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1042239055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6309549290130140047">In the comment thread to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1035513036' rel='nofollow noopener'>the review of Dennett's <i>Breaking the Spell</i></a> which I posted a couple of days ago, much of the discussion has turned on the concept of martyrdom. Dennett argues that religion is a self-reproducing pattern of behavior (a "meme"), and that a martyr is someone who has been taken over by a meme to the point where he is willing to sacrifice his life for his beliefs. Maybe irrational for the martyr, but perfectly rational from the meme's point of view: the history of religion sho</span>
  <span id="freeText6309549290130140047" style="display:none">In the comment thread to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1035513036" rel="nofollow noopener">the review of Dennett's <i>Breaking the Spell</i></a> which I posted a couple of days ago, much of the discussion has turned on the concept of martyrdom. Dennett argues that religion is a self-reproducing pattern of behavior (a "meme"), and that a martyr is someone who has been taken over by a meme to the point where he is willing to sacrifice his life for his beliefs. Maybe irrational for the martyr, but perfectly rational from the meme's point of view: the history of religion shows that martyrdom is an effective way for religions to spread. <br /><br />Dennett draws a sharp distinction between science, which he says is rational and fact-based, and religion, which isn't. It seemed to me at the time that this wasn't so clear; even if a given scientific theory may be rational and fact-based, the scientific world-view itself is as arbitrary as a religious one. I happen to approve of rational, fact-based belief systems, but any attempt I make to justify them will presuppose rationality and facts, so my arguments don't add anything. It's as good, or bad, as a religious person justifying their own world-view by telling me it's the Word of God. But there are some objective differences between science and religion, just viewed as behavioral patterns, and one of these is martyrdom. There are very few people in history whom one could reasonably call martyrs to rationality. Socrates looks like a clear example; but who else is there? Of course, we immediately thought of Galileo. The fact of the matter, though, is that Galileo wasn't martyred. He was threatened by the Inquisition, and he backed down.<br /><br />Why? This is perhaps the central issue in Brecht's play. Brecht does not presents Galileo as a particularly admirable human being. He fraudulently passes off the telescope as his own invention in order to improve his financial position, he ruins his daughter's life by his thoughtless behavior towards her fiancé, and, finally, he exhibits simple cowardice when confronted by the enraged Pope Urban VIII. Even as a scientist, he is by no means above reproach: in the <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i>, his most important work, the central plank of his argument to demonstrate the movement of the Earth rests on an explanation of the tides which is simply wrong. Feyerabend, in <i>Against Method</i>, takes pleasure in making him look like a bungler and near-charlatan, and annoyed many scientists by witnessing against him when the Vatican reopened the case in the late 20th century. <br /><br />But despite all this, Galileo has become one of the most respected people in the history of science, and his influence on its subsequent development is incalculable. In Brecht's version of the story, Galileo doesn't know why he behaves the way he does. His student, learning of the important work on dynamics that he has completed during the last years of his life, wants him to say that he carried out a clever strategic retreat, but Galileo is having none of it. There was no plan; he was just afraid of being tortured. He sounds bitter and sincere. <br /><br />I would be interested to see Dennett's take: from his perspective, the moral of the story is perhaps that memes for rational thought do not spread in the same way as memes for religious conviction. I'm still not sure why that would be, but thinking about this play may help me understand it better. Thank you, Herr Brecht.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6309549290130140047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2298727716" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2298727716" />
    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2298727716?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>
      </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2298727716" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16987473785729595575"><b>Young man, I do not eat my cheese absentmindedly.</b><br/><br/>Despite my perforated memory, I can still cling to triumph, most of which are the achievements of others but alas I can still appreciate. I thought about Brecht at the end of his life this morning while enjoying this masterful narrative. Did he regard himself as recanted? Did his petty tyranny of the women in his life strike him as abominable? Galileo as depicted by Brecht is too pragmatic to be disarmed by such pondering. He is at ease groveling </span>
  <span id="freeText16987473785729595575" style="display:none"><b>Young man, I do not eat my cheese absentmindedly.</b><br /><br />Despite my perforated memory, I can still cling to triumph, most of which are the achievements of others but alas I can still appreciate. I thought about Brecht at the end of his life this morning while enjoying this masterful narrative. Did he regard himself as recanted? Did his petty tyranny of the women in his life strike him as abominable? Galileo as depicted by Brecht is too pragmatic to be disarmed by such pondering. He is at ease groveling for appointments as he understands the alternative. Aside from the necessity of obsequiousness he recognizes the need of discretion and the effects of The Age of Reason not only on the established order but on human existential orientation. He anticipates Weber’s disenchantment but finds solace in wine, bread and conversation. <br /><br />What of my own missteps and absences? As a reader I blunder about with wistful grasps at concepts and reverie. Muddled by self deprecation, labor and lager—somehow I persevere. I needed this play today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16987473785729595575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_191611379" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/191611379" />
    <a title="Aniruddha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5910851-aniruddha"><img alt="Aniruddha" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/191611379?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aniruddha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aniruddha" href="/user/show/5910851-aniruddha">Aniruddha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer191611379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17824728420487142235">There are many reasons why I chose to write about this in my common application to American colleges. This book has had a phenomenal influence on me. I have loved and enjoyed every part of this play. Life of Galileo is not merely another book about the enraging conflict between science and its mindless counterpart religion, but also about society and in the end life itself.<br/><br/>People oft forget that Bertolt Brecht was a Marxist intellectual and his plays not only reflect that but also impersonate hi</span>
  <span id="freeText17824728420487142235" style="display:none">There are many reasons why I chose to write about this in my common application to American colleges. This book has had a phenomenal influence on me. I have loved and enjoyed every part of this play. Life of Galileo is not merely another book about the enraging conflict between science and its mindless counterpart religion, but also about society and in the end life itself.<br /><br />People oft forget that Bertolt Brecht was a Marxist intellectual and his plays not only reflect that but also impersonate his Marxist leanings. It was not Das Kapital that made me a Marxist but rather this play. This play has a bigger theme than just the inquisition of Galileo by the Church- it talks about powers, class struggles, the role of science in society and much more. It is a book about revolutions-not just in science but in society.<br /><br />This book will not appeal to many and I will not recommend it to any reader who wants a controversy-free book. I will not suggest this book to any of my friends who have presuppositions that will hinder their understanding of this book. If you have a scientific bent of mind, if you love to question and love to be questioned. If you hate tradition and don't play chess with pawns but are Bohemian and adventurous to move forward to attack with your best forces, then is your BIBLE. Put simply, if you love freedom, you will love this book. If you believe freedom can be traded for tradition and conservatism has a role to play in our present time, STAY AWAY FROM THE BOOK. Its 5 out of 5, or even 6 out of 5, if you are like me, an open-minded individual who loves science and freedom!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17824728420487142235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806219093" href="/book/show/36481128-vita-di-galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98032241-annie?shelf=owls-2020">owls-2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2851640198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2522244252224304600"><i>English title: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2525182.Life_of_Galileo' title='Life of Galileo by Bertolt Brecht' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life of Galileo</a></i><br/><br/><blockquote>“Unhappy the land that is in need of heroes.”</blockquote><br/><br/>This is a play about Galileo, and at the same time it is not.<br/><br/>Galileo's relationship with the Church is one of the most obvious (and possibly also the most famous) examples of the age-old conflict between science and power, a conflict that at the time this play was written in was very significant; the use of scientific discoveries as means of human annihilation by the ruling class was rampant during WWII. One may be brough</span>
  <span id="freeText2522244252224304600" style="display:none"><i>English title: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2525182.Life_of_Galileo" title="Life of Galileo by Bertolt Brecht" rel="nofollow noopener">Life of Galileo</a></i><br /><br /><blockquote>“Unhappy the land that is in need of heroes.”</blockquote><br /><br />This is a play about Galileo, and at the same time it is not.<br /><br />Galileo's relationship with the Church is one of the most obvious (and possibly also the most famous) examples of the age-old conflict between science and power, a conflict that at the time this play was written in was very significant; the use of scientific discoveries as means of human annihilation by the ruling class was rampant during WWII. One may be brought to think that science should be free from any bond to authority for mankind to benefit from it. <br /><br />Brecht wanted exactly this: to make people critically think about the science-power connection. He gave very specific instructions to represent <i>Life of Galileo</i> on stage. He wanted the audience to be alienated, rather than empathise with any of the characters. People had to be able to look beyond the events happening on stage and see the bigger picture, question the times they were living in. And this is why now <i>Life of Galileo</i>, like all the masterpieces of dramaturgy, sounds as modern and significant as almost a century ago.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2522244252224304600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11334185906701749278" style="display:none">May be three and half stars.<br /><br />As a performance, I think this would have been a five star play. To read, I think something was lacking. There were lengthy dialogues that sounded more preachy for me than dramatic. This is where the rating suffered.<br /><br />But the presentation of the life of Galileo Galilei is complete. He comes alive as a curious scientist, mathematician and most of all a frightened human being at the threat from the Church. He stands for science and human reason. He also portrays that human reason can bend in the face of physical torture.<br /><br />Go for it. It is worth your time and money. You can have the feeling of being in the working studio of Galileo Galilei of 17th century.</span>

<div id="review_879372835" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/879372835" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8067730-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562742838p2/8067730.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/879372835?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/8067730-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105381" href="/book/show/2525182.Life_of_Galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8067730-thomas?shelf=penguin-blacks">penguin-blacks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8067730-thomas?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer879372835" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8664056655334796311"><i>Life of Galileo</i>, by Bertolt Brecht, is a lively and fascinating work about science and moral responsibility. Written less like a play and more in the vein of Socratic dialogue, Brecht offers a concise fictionalised account of the rise and fall of Galileo Galilei. What makes this play truly beautiful is that is does not try to affirm science as filled with wonder and potential, but instead employs a critical negativity to make readers re-evaluate scientific research and its potentially detrimenta</span>
  <span id="freeText8664056655334796311" style="display:none"><i>Life of Galileo</i>, by Bertolt Brecht, is a lively and fascinating work about science and moral responsibility. Written less like a play and more in the vein of Socratic dialogue, Brecht offers a concise fictionalised account of the rise and fall of Galileo Galilei. What makes this play truly beautiful is that is does not try to affirm science as filled with wonder and potential, but instead employs a critical negativity to make readers re-evaluate scientific research and its potentially detrimental consequences, as well as the moral responsibility scientists have to their society and their planet. Enduringly relevant and thoroughly enjoyable, this play should be standard reading for students of all disciplines during their intellectual development.<br /><br />Written with World War and Nuclear Weaponry as a backdrop, <i>Galileo</i> offers a sharp moral tale. Brecht's primary goal is to reinforce an important lesson: Scientists <i>must</i> take moral responsibility for what they create. Scientific research, in Brecht's time as in ours, tended to be conducted for the sake of knowledge rather than for any use of meaningful contribution to society. Worse still, in Brecht's view, scientific research is funded by State institutions and subordinated to the ruling class in both the economy and military. Content enough to take funding and prove their hypotheses, scientists take no heed of the potential catastrophe they can unleash with their researches. This message is as important now as it was in Brecht's age. Whilst the playwright lived in the shadow of nuclear annihilation, wrought by the Manhattan Project, we too live in the shadow of a great cataclysm: global warming. For the present era, the desperate search for meaning and for a solution to the problem means the moral tale in this play touches close to home. So too does the subordination of science to the ruling (capitalist) classes. Every day new consumer products, often running on fossil fuel or else consuming electricity and thus reproducing the need for oil burning, are released on to the market. Profits soar, investors get rich and capital expands across the globe - concentrated inexorably in the hands of a small, plutocratic elite. Similarly, the research and solutions for global warming remain broadly neglected for the sake of reproducing the capitalist economy. All the while, scientists in the high castle of the university continue to enable these conditions, more concerned with producing knowledge and testing theories than with the practical consequences for society and the planet. For Brecht, this would be unacceptable. For us, this means <i>Galileo</i> remains incredibly important and touches a nerve close to home.<br /><br />An important element of this play is the way the viewer and reader understand the characters. The reader of the text is not supposed to connect with any character, and Brecht is careful to alienate his characters from his readers. Rather, he wants the reader to be pulled 'out of a passive and unconscious state of mind and into a heightened condition of awareness that leads to an alternative way of thinking.' In short, Brecht does not allow his characters to be endeared to a reader because he wants a reader to think critically and at a distance from the world Galileo and his companions inhabit. The characters are all reprehensible in their own way, Galileo especially. When a reader sees Galileo saying that he 'abjure[s] what I have taught...I foreswear, detest and curse, with sincere heart and unfeigned faith, all these errors and heresies,' they are filled with disgust and moral outrage. What Galileo has done is the most immoral act he could have committed. Not even his excuse that he was 'afraid of physical pain' engenders sympathy. Instead, it makes it infinitely worse; Galileo did not think that Truth was worth the stake, and instead gave in to the petty prejudices of the body. The reader loathes him because of what he purported to be, and hates him even more for what he turned out to be: a coward and a weak human being.<br /><br />This human element is important for Brecht's depiction of Galileo. He does not want a reader to mislead by the ideal notion of the scientist. Galileo is not a soul functioning solely on reason, driven by the ideal of Truth and willing to give anything and everything for it. Despite his claims that he believes 'in Human Reason' wholeheartedly, Galileo is presented as a servant to his stomach and to his body. Brecht manages this depiction with excellent subtlety, a particularly malicious depiction coming in when Galileo lies about his researches into the telescope - he found out that morning it was mass produced in a foreign land - to fool his benefactors into giving him a pay rise. Here Galileo thinks only for his material needs, willing to lie and obscure Truth for his own selfish ends. This reaches fruition later, when Ludivico remarks that Galileo 'will always be the slave of [his] passions.' Brecht is here making a throwaway reference to the Scottish philosopher David Hume who posits 'Reason is, and ought only to be the slave of the passions, and can never pretend to any other office than to serve and obey them.' What reason is, for Hume, is a tool and process to help a person get what they desire. In Galileo this is profoundly the case. He is not pure reason and intelligence functioning with the aim of discovering Truth. Instead, he is just a man prepared to use his mind to get what he wants, and a man prepared to throw Truth away simply to avoid physical pain. Galileo's fickle material interests sicken the reader and drive home the important and oft forgotten point that scientists are also just human, and their funding is as much to satisfy their wants as it is to be used to discover the Truth.<br /><br />The moral imperative that Brecht desires to drive home forms a central allure of this play, as does the depiction of scientists as humans with material wants and needs. Brecht forcefully reminds his readers that everything in this world comes at a price, and that everything someone does has consequences they might not be prepared to accept. Regardless, Brecht correctly maintains that people – scientists, philosophers or otherwise – must take responsibility for what they create. A truly great scientist is one with a social conscience that carefully considers and weighs the potential outcomes of their actions and researches. A great scientist, for Brecht, does not simply take money from the State and the capitalist class for the sake of Truth, but carefully considers what good or bad outcomes for their society the results of their researches have. <i>Life of Galileo</i> should very much be standard reading for scientists, philosophers and everyone engaged in the search for, and production of, Truth. It must not be forgotten that what is produced, what is learned and what is known have potentially great, and potentially dire and catastrophic, consequences for society at large. What makes Brecht great is that he not only does not forget it, he makes sure this message in encased in a stylistically and aesthetically beautiful piece of literature that will endure through the cultural history of humankind and remain as urgently relevant through the ages as it was when it was written.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8664056655334796311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_483204931" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/483204931" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4351439-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319242813p2/4351439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/483204931?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/4351439-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783518100011" href="/book/show/241526.Leben_des_Galilei">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4351439-seth?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer483204931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8523289915266276447">In the “Life of Galileo,” Bertolt Brecht highlights the astronomer’s struggles with the Catholic Church, which opposed his work extending the theories of Copernicus and threatened him with torture. This was not an idle threat since the Church actually burned at the stake the heliocentric astronomer Giordano Bruno in 1600. It is interesting how leading clerics refused his offer to gaze through his primitive telescope, instead insisting on a philosophical dispute, preferably in Latin, about whethe</span>
  <span id="freeText8523289915266276447" style="display:none">In the “Life of Galileo,” Bertolt Brecht highlights the astronomer’s struggles with the Catholic Church, which opposed his work extending the theories of Copernicus and threatened him with torture. This was not an idle threat since the Church actually burned at the stake the heliocentric astronomer Giordano Bruno in 1600. It is interesting how leading clerics refused his offer to gaze through his primitive telescope, instead insisting on a philosophical dispute, preferably in Latin, about whether what he claimed to see could be true. They challenged him to explain why he knew more than God when he wrote the Bible and more than the philosopher Aristotle when he wrote about astronomy. The refusal to accept new evidence is an example of the anti-scientific and anti-modern mindset. Under pressure not to challenge the geocentric Ptolemaic model propagated by the Church, Galileo maintained public silence for eight years. Finally, in 1633, Pope Urban VIII forced him to recant. Galileo spent the rest of his life under house arrest. However, he surreptitiously managed to write his "Discursi Mathematica," which, when smuggled out of Italy, formed the basis of modern physics. <br /><br />The play features some exceptionally beautiful language. Here are a few aphorisms from the chapter headings with my translations:<br /><br />In dem Jahr sechzehnhundertundneun<br />Schien das Licht des Wissens hell<br />Zu Padua aus einem kleinen Haus.<br />Galileo Galilei rechnete aus:<br />Die Sonn steht still, die Erd kommt von der Stell.<br /><br />In the year sixteen hundred nine<br />The light of knowledge shone brightly<br />In Padua from a small house.<br />Galileo Galilei calculated: <br />The sun stands still, the Earth moves.<br /><br />--<br /><br />Sechzehnhunderzehn, zehnter Januar<br />Galileo Galilei sah, daß kein Himmel war.<br /><br />Sixteen hundred ten, the tenth of January<br />Galileo Galilei saw there was no heaven.<br /><br />--<br /><br />In Rom war Galileo Galilei Gast<br />In einem Kardinalspalast.<br />Man bot ihm Schmaus und bot ihm Wein<br />Und hatt’ nur ein klein Wünschelein.<br /><br />In Rome Galileo Galilei was a guest<br />In a Cardinal’s palace.<br />They offered him a feast and wine<br />And had just a very small wish.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8523289915266276447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1193758050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1193758050" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332087637p2/8255719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1193758050?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0413577805" href="/book/show/910769.Life_of_Galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1193758050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13997092822512612648">It’s not often that I read plays. There’s a couple of reasons for this: living in London, I prefer to go and see them performed whenever possible, but also I’ve never really thought of drama as a medium that I’d frequently sit down and just <i>read</i> like I would a book. It’s a strange, stupid prejudice on my part which probably has something to do with the fact that I have no interest in writing drama either. I don't particularly enjoy dialogue, and whenever I read a script I always feel dragged dow</span>
  <span id="freeText13997092822512612648" style="display:none">It’s not often that I read plays. There’s a couple of reasons for this: living in London, I prefer to go and see them performed whenever possible, but also I’ve never really thought of drama as a medium that I’d frequently sit down and just <i>read</i> like I would a book. It’s a strange, stupid prejudice on my part which probably has something to do with the fact that I have no interest in writing drama either. I don't particularly enjoy dialogue, and whenever I read a script I always feel dragged down by all the necessary formal elements; with a few exceptions, the result often seems inhibited on the page by conditions which a skilful director would render invisible (or beautiful!) on-stage.<br /><br />I read this because it was lying around, and because I’ve never read any Brecht before. This is strange because I did English at university where his name was frequently thrown around, usually in the contexts of literary theory, and it’s quite easy to get by (even at a postgraduate level) with only a second-hand knowledge of a subject you bring up in passing. Anyway, all of this is only to say that I knew a little bit about the author before I began. It’s not an especially long play, so I read it in a few sittings over the course of a weekend. <br /><br />It’s really quite good, and I don’t have an awful lot to say about it. The plot is exactly what the title suggests, and presents us with scenes from the life of the astronomer Galileo with an emphasis on his discovery of the telescope, and the development of his argument that the earth moves around the sun in a system full of other stars and planets and moons doing much the same thing. This got him into some trouble with the Catholic church, and eventually he was forced to publicly recant his theories, which were contrary to the teachings of the time. <br /><br />The author fits this well-known story into a context that might have seemed familiar to an audience of his contemporaries. Renaissance Italy is characterised as a world of many small revolutions happening everywhere, all the time; some of these are explicitly political, but most just seem to come about as a matter of ordinary people working and striving to find better ways to live. In this context, Galileo hardly seems so extraordinary, but to his rulers his ideas are dangerous. If those mythical crystal spheres which hold the heavens in order are to be shattered, and that perfect order with the pope at the centre of the universe is to be abolished, then what other institutions could not also fall? <br /><br />There’s also a great deal about the role of science, and the importance of humanist reason and rationality in a world full of superstition. This aspect is tinged with sadness and doubt; there’s the implication throughout that Galileo may be deluded in his relentless portrayal of objective truth, perhaps even naive in his belief that man is essentially a rational creature. (Apparently the author went back and rewrote parts of this play after Hiroshima.)<br /><br />I’m going to venture out on a limb and suggest that the finest moments here are when the author seems to forget he is supposed to be writing a play — a thing with characters, drama, dialogue, context and setting, etc — and starts simply <i>writing</i>. A character will command the attention of the audience in monologue and will explain themselves in a way which is wholly contrived but utterly fascinating. The author has the remarkable gift for making an argument which one can find totally compelling, even if you don’t find it actually convincing or believable or correct or true. <br /><br />(Most of what he has to say <i>is</i> true, though.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13997092822512612648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1897628779" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1897628779" />
    <a title="Layla (Between the Lines)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50914935-layla-between-the-lines"><img alt="Layla (Between the Lines)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604470920p2/50914935.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1897628779?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Layla (Between the Lines)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Layla (Between the Lines)" href="/user/show/50914935-layla-between-the-lines">Layla (Between the Lines)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413577801" href="/book/show/25211749-life-of-galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50914935-layla-between-the-lines?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1897628779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5482724425477560078">I thoroughly enjoyed this play! I have to admit that this is a pleasant surprise because I normally dread the books I'm assigned to read for university. Since this is part of my required reading (and I have to write a lot of about it in class), I am not going to write a full review for it here. <b>Instead, here are some of my favourite quotes from the text:</b> <blockquote>
  <i>“Listen to me: someone who doesn't know the truth is just thick-headed. But someone who does know it and calls it a lie is a crook.”<br/><br/>“...I be</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText5482724425477560078" style="display:none">I thoroughly enjoyed this play! I have to admit that this is a pleasant surprise because I normally dread the books I'm assigned to read for university. Since this is part of my required reading (and I have to write a lot of about it in class), I am not going to write a full review for it here. <b>Instead, here are some of my favourite quotes from the text:</b> <blockquote>
  <i>“Listen to me: someone who doesn't know the truth is just thick-headed. But someone who does know it and calls it a lie is a crook.”<br /><br />“...I believe in Humanity, which means to say I believe in human reason. If it weren't for that belief each morning I wouldn't have the power to get out of bed.”<br /><br />“Thinking is one of the greatest pleasures of the human race.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="5482724425477560078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_13395493" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13395493" />
    <a title="Robert Beveridge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/766524-robert-beveridge"><img alt="Robert Beveridge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286387835p2/766524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13395493?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Beveridge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Beveridge" href="/user/show/766524-robert-beveridge">Robert Beveridge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/766524-robert-beveridge?shelf=finished">finished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/766524-robert-beveridge?shelf=owned-and-gave-away">owned-and-gave-away</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13395493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer881030618899323304">Bertolt Brecht, Galileo (Grove Press, 1952)<br/><br/>Publishers who put out "literature" (perhaps I should capitalize the L) have felt it necessary for the past half-century or so to include long-winded dissections of the texts as a part of their editions. No mind is paid, seemingly, to whether these long-winded dissections contain major plot spoilers (they almost always do). Add Eric Bentley's interminable preface to the Grove Press edition of Brecht's Galileo to the list. Perhaps Grove assumes anyone re</span>
  <span id="freeText881030618899323304" style="display:none">Bertolt Brecht, Galileo (Grove Press, 1952)<br /><br />Publishers who put out "literature" (perhaps I should capitalize the L) have felt it necessary for the past half-century or so to include long-winded dissections of the texts as a part of their editions. No mind is paid, seemingly, to whether these long-winded dissections contain major plot spoilers (they almost always do). Add Eric Bentley's interminable preface to the Grove Press edition of Brecht's Galileo to the list. Perhaps Grove assumes anyone reading the thing will either have already read the play or will be so turned off by Belntley's wooden prose style that they won't read far enough to get to the spoilers. My advice: go the second route. And book publishers, if you're putting essays in your editions, PLEASE put them AFTER the actual text, so the novice reader of a given work will be able to approach it without the coloring of another reader's analysis.<br /><br />Bentley spends forty-odd pages discussing the historical inaccuracies of Brecht's Galileo and the two extant versions of the text (though Bentley says both are presented in the Grive edition, this is not the case; from his comments, I gather this is the second version of the play, completed after WW2 [the first was completed in 1937]). Bentley goes on forever about the socialist qualities of Galileo, and whether the scientist makes a worthy Marxist hero, both in the reader's eyes and in Brecht's. Whether anyone outside those writing a paper for a Marxist lit class would care doesn't seem to have crossed his mind. Brecht is one of the few authors who is capable of taking a political statement and couching it in such writing as to make the statement itself visible only to those looking for it; Galileo's Marxism, or lack of same, doesn't hit the reader in the face with a dead herring (or a dropped pebble, as 'twere) throughout the text. Commendable, especially for as fervent a Marxist as was Brecht. Here is a man who never let the message overtake the medium, and scads of modern authors could do with repeated readings of this text to get a handle on what it is they're doing wrong.<br /><br />Bentley aside, the play itself is certainly worth the reader's time. Galileo is presented from the time of his first findings with which Mother Church took offense until twenty years after his recantation. While the play mainly focuses on Galileo and how his own views toward his work affect him and those around him, we're not allowed to go away without understanding how those views also affected the Italian society around him; as with all things, the subversion to be found in Galileo's discovery that the Earth revolves around the Sun instead of vice-versa seeps into the public mind, much to the Church's dismay. But at its heart, the play is about the man himself and those around him. Galileo himself, historically accurate or not, is a convincing character, and his family, friends, and supporters are also very well-drawn (with the arguable exception of his daughter, who never seems to really flesh out and become a believable human being; her actions and reactions are predictable and wooden). Whatever the message underlying, and whether the reader agrees with it or not, Galileo is first and foremost a decent piece of drama. Leave Bentley's preface until after you've drawn your own conclusions. ** 1/2 (**** for the play, zero for Bentley's comments) </span>
  <a data-text-id="881030618899323304" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1535016170" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1535016170" />
    <a title="Victor Davis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31656053-victor-davis"><img alt="Victor Davis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457452123p2/31656053.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1535016170?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victor Davis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victor Davis" href="/user/show/31656053-victor-davis">Victor Davis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105381" href="/book/show/2525182.Life_of_Galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31656053-victor-davis?shelf=influences">influences</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1535016170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7741970564927515663">What a powerful play. I've always heard the adage that plays are meant to be performed, not read. That's fine, but I still can't shake the belief that the most substantive plays must be written by substantive playwrights who must have just as masterful a command over the written word as the visual performance. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26853.Bertolt_Brecht' title='Bertolt Brecht' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bertolt Brecht</a> possesses that command. I've always loved <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17250.The_Crucible' title='The Crucible by Arthur Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Crucible</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman' title='Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death of a Salesman</a>, and recently added <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22327.The_Miracle_Worker' title='The Miracle Worker by William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Miracle Worker</a> to my list of favorite plays, but even these undisputed</span>
  <span id="freeText7741970564927515663" style="display:none">What a powerful play. I've always heard the adage that plays are meant to be performed, not read. That's fine, but I still can't shake the belief that the most substantive plays must be written by substantive playwrights who must have just as masterful a command over the written word as the visual performance. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26853.Bertolt_Brecht" title="Bertolt Brecht" rel="nofollow noopener">Bertolt Brecht</a> possesses that command. I've always loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17250.The_Crucible" title="The Crucible by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crucible</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12898.Death_of_a_Salesman" title="Death of a Salesman by Arthur Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of a Salesman</a>, and recently added <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22327.The_Miracle_Worker" title="The Miracle Worker by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Miracle Worker</a> to my list of favorite plays, but even these undisputed masters left so much in the hands of the production that the dialog seems rushed compared to ordinary prose. Not this play. The dialog itself is cleverly presented in dialectic form, with tragically heroic Galileo entrapping characters into Platonic question-answer sessions throughout the book. Thus Brecht uses the very principle he seeks to elevate, namely, that scientific thought is intuitive and morally right.<br /><br />I've read in plenty of science books about how the medieval church didn't care so much whether the sun went round the earth so much as the idea of the common man using mortal instruments and mortal reason to divine <i>immortal</i> truths. They were worried the telescope could put them out of the job. This story illustrates just that concept. The enemy was not superstition or tradition but authority. The scientific revolution, like so many enlightenment movements, had as much to do with throwing off oppression as seeking truth. Both share the same driving motivation: of looking at the same world and seeing it brighter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7741970564927515663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5001988177480835044">One of the very many quotes that I utterly enjoyed from this play:<br/><br/>"SAGREDO: Galileo, I see you embarking on a frightful road. It is a disastrous night when mankind sees the truth. And a delusive hour when it believes in human reason. What kind of person is said to go into things with his eyes open? One who is going to his doom. How could the people in power give free rein to somebody who knows the truth, even if it concerns the remotest stars? Do you imagine the Pope will hear the truth when you</span>
  <span id="freeText5001988177480835044" style="display:none">One of the very many quotes that I utterly enjoyed from this play:<br /><br />"SAGREDO: Galileo, I see you embarking on a frightful road. It is a disastrous night when mankind sees the truth. And a delusive hour when it believes in human reason. What kind of person is said to go into things with his eyes open? One who is going to his doom. How could the people in power give free rein to somebody who knows the truth, even if it concerns the remotest stars? Do you imagine the Pope will hear the truth when you tell him he's wrong, and not just hear that he's wrong? Do you imagine he will merely not in his diary: January 10th, 1610--got rid of heaven? How can you propose to leave the Republic with the truth in your pocket, risking the traps set by monks and princes and brandishing your tube. You may be a sceptic in science, but you're childishly credulous as soon as anything seems likely to help you pursue it. You don't believe in Aristotle, but you do believe in the Grand Duke of Florence. Just now, when I was watching you at the telescope and you were watching those new stars, it seemed to me I was watching you stand on blazing faggots; and when you said you believed in proof I smelt burnt flesh. I am fond of science, my friend, but I am fonder of you. Don't go to Florence, Galileo."</span>

<div id="review_759068837" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/759068837" />
    <a title="Maria Nicolaou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15858149-maria-nicolaou"><img alt="Maria Nicolaou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515014882p2/15858149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/759068837?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria Nicolaou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria Nicolaou" href="/user/show/15858149-maria-nicolaou">Maria Nicolaou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413763808" href="/book/show/64413.Life_of_Galileo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer759068837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7428529252122712142">Brecht's Galileo is one of the most realistic, fully developed characters I've experienced so far. The author does not "contaminate" his work with exaggerations, and does not hesitate to add a little humour in what might have been a tragedy. And this is why he gives heroism a new meaning; to be a hero does not mean to sacrifice one's life. It is rather the kind of heroism that sacrifices the anticipations of people likes us: people who are so eager to grant heroism a taste of feverish revolution</span>
  <span id="freeText7428529252122712142" style="display:none">Brecht's Galileo is one of the most realistic, fully developed characters I've experienced so far. The author does not "contaminate" his work with exaggerations, and does not hesitate to add a little humour in what might have been a tragedy. And this is why he gives heroism a new meaning; to be a hero does not mean to sacrifice one's life. It is rather the kind of heroism that sacrifices the anticipations of people likes us: people who are so eager to grant heroism a taste of feverish revolution, people like Andrea in the book. I believe that the singularity of this exemplary work is exemplified in two lines between Andrea and Galileo: <br />"Unhappy the land that has no heroes."<br />"No. Unhappy the land where heroes are needed."<br /><br />Through Brecht's Galileo the reader enters the caverns of reality which have been there for us, ready, shaped, but untouched; and perhaps it is through this "new" heroism that we will open our eyes to possibility, truth, and revolution: the revolution of the mind, and not of the heart. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7428529252122712142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
